Comment

Found also in P─Бipp. xvi.тАФ*тМКVerses 9 and 28 are triс╣гс╣нubh, the remaining 29 being exceptions!тМЛ

тМКThe prat─лka coincides with that of xi. 6. 9, bhav─Б┼Ыarv─Б╠Бv id├бm br┼лmaс╕е, and that of iv. 28, bh├бv─Б┼Ыarv─Бu manv├й v─Бm: see introduction to the latter. The only quotation in V─Бit. is at 29. 10, where the hymn accompanies an offering to Rudra: and it is accordingly reckoned to the r─Бudra gaс╣Зa (note to K─Бu┼Ы. 50. 13). Verse 31 is reckoned to the abhaya gaс╣Зa (note to K─Бu┼Ы. 16. 8). Further citations in K─Бu┼Ы. are as follows: the hymn is used (129. 3) with an oblation in deprecating an evil omen; D─Бr─лla understands it as meant at 28. 8 (see introd. to iv. 28); Ke┼Ыava and the comm. hold that it is to be used with a dozen other hymns in a rite (50. 13-14) for safety on a business journey; Ke┼Ыava (not the comm.) takes it to be intended with v. 6 at 51. 7 in a rite for the safety of the cattle.тАФAccording to Caland’s interpretation of yuktayos at 50. 17, it is to be used (with vi. 128) in the rite there prescribed for keeping snakes etc. from house and field; but perhaps iii. 26 and 27 are rather intended (see my introduction to iii. 26).тМЛ

Whitney
Translation
  1. O Bhava-and-┼Ыarva, be gracious; do not go against [us]; ye lords of
    beings, lords of cattle, homage to you! [the arrow] that is fitted,
    that is drawn, do not let fly; do not harm our bipeds nor quadrupeds.
Notes

The comm. first explains m─Б╠Б in a as if it were m─Б, object of
abh├н y─Бtam, and then, alternatively, in its proper sense. тМКFor
─Б╠Бyata, see note to vi. 65. 1.тМЛ

Griffith

Bhava and Sarva, spare us, be not hostile. Homage to you, twin Lords of beasts and spirits! Shoot not the arrow aimed and drawn against us: forbear to harm our quadrupeds and bipeds.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Make ye not bodies for the dog, the jackal, for the buzzards (?
    al├нklava), the vultures, and them that are black [and] greedy
    (aviс╣гy├║); let thy flies, lord of cattle, let thy birds not find
    themselves at food.
Notes

‘Bodies’ (┼Ыar─лra) must be taken here in the sense of ‘dead bodies.’
The accent of k├бrtam is, though rather strange, not indefensible, as
in the former of two parallel clauses; the comm. reads instead kartum*.*
At├нklava is found only here and in 9. 9, and is rendered purely
conjecturally; the comm. reads instead aviklabebhyas, and Ppp.
ariklavebhyas. All the pada-mss. separate m─Б╠Бvidanta at the end
into m─Б╠Б: avidanta; SPP., in his pada-text, makes, with the comm.,
the necessary emendation to vid-. The construction and sense of d
are obscure and doubtful; Ppp. has a wholly different reading: vi┼Ыase
m─Б vi┼Ыyantu.

Griffith

Cast not our bodies to the dog or jackal, nor, Lord of Beasts! to carrion-kites or vultures. Let not thy black voracious flies attack them; let not thy birds obtain them for their banquet.

(рддреЗ) рддреЗрд░реА [рдЙрддреНрдкрдиреНрди] (рдордХреНрд╖рд┐рдХрд╛рдГ) рдордХреНрдЦрд┐рдпрд╛рдБ рдФрд░ (рддреЗ) рддреЗрд░реЗ [рдЙрддреНрдкрдиреНрди] (рд╡рдпрд╛рдВрд╕рд┐) рдкрдХреНрд╖реА (рд╡рд┐рдШрд╕реЗ) рднреЛрдЬрди рдкрд░ (рдорд╛ рд╡рд┐рджрдиреНрдд) [рд╣рдореЗрдВ] рди рдкреНрд░рд╛рдкреНрдд рд╣реЛрд╡реЗрдВ реереирее

рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рдд рдХреНрд╖реЗрдордХрд░рдгрджрд╛рд╕ рддреНрд░рд┐рд╡реЗрджреА - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

Whitney
Translation
  1. Unto thy noise (? kr├бnda), [thy] breath, and what pangs (?
    r├│pi) are thine, O BhavaтАФhomage we pay to thee that art thousand-eyed,
    O Rudra, immortal one.
Notes

The comm. glosses krand─Бya with krandan─Бya ┼Ыab─Бdya, and ropayas
with ropayitryo mohayitryas tanvaс╕е; he reads at the end amartyas,
explaining it as used for a dative.

Griffith

We offer homage to thy shout, Bhava! thy breath, thy racking pains: Homage, Immortal One! to thee, to Rudra of the thousand eyes.

Whitney
Translation
  1. We pay thee homage in front, above, also below; forth from the sphere
    of the sky, homage [be] to thine atmosphere.
Notes

The comm. explains abh─лvarg├б as = avak─Б┼Ы─Бtmaka ─Бk─Б┼Ыaс╕е. The verse is
mostly wanting in Ppp.

Griffith

We offer reverence to thee from eastward, and from north and south, From all the compass of the sky, to thee and to the firmament.

Whitney
Translation
  1. With the blue-locked archer, the thousand-eyed, vigorous, with Rudra,
    the half-smiter (?)тАФwith him may we not come into collision (sam-с╣Ы).
Notes

Ardhaka-gh─Бtin, in c, is met with only here, and is of obscure
meaning; the comm. says sen─Бy─Б ardhaс╣Б hantuс╣Б ┼Ы─лlam asya, i.e. ‘able to
destroy half an army at once.’ No variant is reported from Ppp. тМКin the
Collation: but in his Notes, Roth does report adhvaga-тМЛ; the minor Pet
Lex. says “Ppp. adhvaga-” and itself conjectures andhaka-. тМКCf. the
notes of Henry, Griffith, Bloomfield. The Kaс╣нha reading, however, should
now be taken into account; and that has in fact adhvaga-: see
Kaс╣нha-hss., p. 15тБ╡.тМЛ Ppp. has at the end samar─Бmasi.

Griffith

Never may we contend with him, the mighty archer, thousand- eyed. Rudra who wears black tufts of hair, the slaughterer of Ardhaka.

режрео рд╕ рдиреЛ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Let this Bhava avoid us on every side; as fire the waters, let Bhava
    avoid us; let him not plot against us; homage be to him.
Notes

Ppp. reads ─Бp─Бi тАЩv─Б тАЩgniс╣г pari in b, and combines no abhi in
c. The comm. has in c the regular form maс╣Еsta; but long ─Б in
this tense occurs a couple of times in other texts also.

Griffith

May he, may Bhava from all sides avoid us, avoid us even as fire avoids the waters. Let him not threaten us. To him be homage!

Griffith

Four times, eight times be homage paid to Bhava, yea, Lord of Beasts, ten times be reverence paid thee! Thine are these animals, five several classes, oxen, and goats and sheep, and men, and horses

Whitney
Translation
  1. Thine are the four directions, thine the heaven, thine the earth,
    thine, O formidable one, this wide atmosphere, thine is all this that
    has life (─Бtm├бn), that is breathing upon (├бnu) the earth.
Notes

Ppp. omits tava pс╣Ыthiv─л, thus rectifying the meter; and it has for
d yad ejad adhi bh┼лmy─Бm.

Griffith

Thine the four regions, thine are earth and heaven, thine, Mighty One, this firmament between them; Thine everything with soul and breath here on the surface of the land.

Whitney
Translation
  1. This wide vessel, holder of good things, is thine, within which are
    all these beings; do thou be gracious to us, O lord of cattle; homage to
    thee; away let the jackals, the portents (abhibh─Б╠Б), the dogs go, away
    the weepers of evil with disheveled hair.
Notes

The comm. identifies the vessel (ko┼Ыa) with the aс╣Зс╕Нakaс╣н─Бha, the
shell of the world-egg. He takes abhibh─Б╠Бs as = abhibhavit─Бras, and
epithet of kroс╣гс╣н─Бras, and agharudas as = aman╠Дgalaс╣Б yath─Б bhavati
tath─Б rodanaс╣Б kurvatyaс╕е; тМКbut see viii. i. 19 and referencesтМЛ. тМКThe
meter of b would be rectified by reading v├н┼Ыv─Бni in place of im─Б╠Б
v├н┼Ыv─Б.тМЛ

Griffith

Thine is this ample wealth-containing storehouse that holds with- in it all these living creatures. Favour us, Lord of Beasts, to thee be homage! Far from us go ill-omens, dogs, and jackals, and wild-haired women with their horrid shrieking!

Translation
  1. Thou bearest a yellow golden bow, a thousand-slaying,
    hundred-weaponed, O tufted one; Rudra’s arrow goes, a god-missile; to

Griffith

A yellow bow of gold thou wieldest, slaying its hundred, tufted God! smiting its thousand. Weapon of Gods, far flies the shaft of Rudra: wherever it may be, we pay it homage.

Whitney
Translation
  1. He who, attacked (abhi-y─Б), hides himself, [who] tries to put
    thee down, O Rudra, him from behind thou pursuest, like the tracker (?
    padan─л╠Б) of one that is pierced.
Notes

With the last p─Бda compare x. 1. 26 b; the expression is apparently
a familiar or proverbial one: тМКcf. Manu viii. 44, ‘as the hunter follows
the track (padaс╣Б nayati) of a deer by the drops of blood’; also
Dhammapada, vss. 179, 180тМЛ. Ppp. reads ugra instead of rudra in
b.

Griffith

Thou, Rudra, followest close the foe who lies in wait to conquer thee. Even as a hunter who pursues the footsteps of the wounded game.

Whitney
Translation
  1. With the thousand-eyed one, seeing across in front, with Rudra,
    hurling in many places, inspired one, may we not come in collision, as
    he goes about (─л╠Бya-) with the tongue.
Notes

The comm. paraphrases atipa┼Ыy├бm with ati┼Ыayen─Б тАЩtikramya v─Б pa┼Ыyati
(the word is omitted in both Pet. Lexx.), and connects purast─Бt either
with it or with asyantam; jihv├бy├й тАЭyam─Бnam he explains as
jihv─Бgreс╣Зa kс╣Ыtsnaс╣Б jagad vy─Бpnuvantam bhakс╣гaс╣З─Бrthaс╣Б lihantam, which is
rather absurd; perhaps jihvay─Б (so Ludwig) belongs rather to ‘we’:
‘we, by what we say.’

Griffith

Let us not outrage with our tongue far-seeing Rudra, thousand- eyed, Inspired with varied lore, who shoots his arrows forward, far away.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Do not let fly at us the club (? maty├а), the god-missile; be not
    angry at us, O lord of cattle; homage to thee; elsewhere than [over]
    us shake out the heavenly bough.
Notes

The bough, namely (so it would seem), from which the portents that fall
from the sky appear to be shaken by a hostile divinity. The comm. reads
martyam instead of matyam in a. He recognizes that sr─Бs (i.e.
sr─Б[kс╣г]s) is from root sс╣Ыj ("= vi sс╣Ыja “). Ppp. has sr─Б m.
devahitam in a.

Griffith

Cast not thy club at us, thy heavenly weapon. Lord of Beasts, be not wroth with us. Let reverence be paid to thee. Shake thy celestial branch above some others elsewhere, not o’er us.

Griffith

Covet not thou our kine or men, covet not thou our goats or sheep. Elsewhither, strong One! turn thine aim: destroy the mockers’ family.

Translation

    non-sacrificing, the god-mockersтАФto him be homage with the ten clever
    ones (┼Ы├бkvar─л).

glosses the word with an╠Дgulibhis, as = karmasu ┼Ыakt─Бbhiс╕е. Ppp.
begins yas tiс╣гс╣нhati vi┼Ыvabhс╣Ыto antarikс╣гe тАШyajvanaс╣г pra-.

Griffith

Homage be paid him with ten Sakvari verses who stands established in the air’s mid-region, slaying non-sacrificing God-despisers!

Translation
  1. To thee are assigned the forest animals тМКpa┼Ы├║тМЛ, the wild beasts in
    the woods, the geese (haс╣Еs├б), eagles, hawks, birds; thine, O lord of
    cattle, is the monster (? yakс╣г├б) within the waters; for thine increase
    flow the waters of the heaven.
Notes

Ppp. reads, for b, tubhyaс╣Б vay─Бс╣Еsi ┼Ыakun─Бс╣г patatriс╣Зaс╕е, elides the
a of apsu in c, and combines divy─Б тАЭpo in d. The comm. has
mс╣Ыdhe at the end, explaining it as = undan─Бya. He takes yakс╣гam as
= p┼лjyaс╣Б svar┼лpam, but does not give any reason why, etymological or
other. тМКOur a is nearly xii. 1. 49 a (with the same redundancy
of a dissyllable), and b is precisely xii. 1. 51 b: for
pa┼Ыavas, cf. also iii. 31. 3, xi. 5. 21, and iii. 10. 6 note.тМЛ

Griffith

For thee were forest beasts and sylvan creatures placed in the wood, and small birds, swans, and eagles. Floods, Lord of Beasts! contain thy living beings: to swell thy strength flow the celestial Waters.

Whitney
Translation
  1. The dolphins (┼Ыiс╣Е┼Ыum─Б╠Бra), boas (ajagar├б), pur─лk├бyas, jash├бs,
    fishes, rajas├бs, at which thou hurlest: there is no distance for thee
    nor hindrance for thee, O Bhava; at once thou loolcest over the whole
    earth; from the eastern thou smitest in the northern ocean.
Notes

Ppp. begins si┼Ыum─Бr─Б ‘jagar─Бс╕е pur─лс╣гay─Б jag─Б mat-. The comm. has
pul─лkay─Бs (like MS.); he takes rajas─Б╠Б (p. -s─Б╠Бс╕е) as if it were the
instr. sing, r├бjas─Б; he passes jaс╣г─Б╠Бs and m├бtsy─Бs without mention,
but defines ┼Ыiс╣Е┼Ыum─Бra as ‘a kind of crocodile (nakra)’ and ajagara
as ‘a kind of serpent.’ For jaс╣г─Б╠Бs, some of the mss. (including our
Bp.P.M.W.) have jakh─Б╠Бs, one or two (including our I.) have jagh─Б╠Бs,
and one of SPP’s jhaс╣г─Б╠Бs; doubtless it is the sea-monster called later
jhaс╣гa. Nearly all the mss. have s├бrv─Бn in d (only our B. тМКand
D.Kp.?тМЛ and two of SPP’s s├бrv─Бс╣Б), and SPP’s text accordingly admits
it, though it seems an evident error, and the comm. reads -v─Бm. Most
of the pada-mss. resolve pariс╣гс╣нh─Б╠Бsti into -sth─Б╠Б: ├бsti (instead of
asti). We are surprised to find a ’northern’ ocean spoken of, and set
over against the ’eastern’ one тМКcf. xi. 5. 6тМЛ, but ├║ttara cannot well
mean anything else. Consistency requires the reading -smint sam- in
e, but the t is accidentally omitted in our text, and SPP’s also
leaves it out.

Griffith

Porpoises, serpents, strange aquatic monsters, fishes, and things unclean at which thou shootest. Nothing is far for thee, naught checks thee, Bhava! The whole earth in a moment thou surveyest. From the east sea thou smitest in the northern.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Do not, O Rudra, unite (sam-sс╣Ыj) us with the takm├бn, not with
    poison, not with the fire of heaven; elsewhere than [on] us make that
    lightning fall.
Notes

The comm. again correctly paraphrases saс╣Б sr─Бс╕е with saс╣Б sс╣Ыja.

Griffith

O’erwhelm us not with Fever or with poison, nor, Rudra! with the fire that comes from heaven. Elsewhere, and not on us, cast down this lightning.

Whitney
Translation
  1. O king Bhava, be gracious to the sacrificer, for thou hast become
    cattle-lord of cattle; whoever has faith, saying “the gods are,” be thou

Griffith

King Bhava, favour him who offers worship, for thou art Pasupati, Lord of victims. Be gracious to the quadruped and biped of the believer in the Gods’ existence.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Not our great one, and not our small, not our carrying one, and not
    those that will carry, not our father and mother do thou harm; our own
    self (tan┼л╠Б), O Rudra, do not injure.
Notes

The verse occurs also as RV. i. 114. 7; VS. xvi. 15; TS. iv. 5. 10┬▓; TA.

b; vadh─лs for hiс╣Еs─лs and m├│ тАЩt├б m─Бt├бram for m─Бt├бraс╣Б ca in
c; and, for d, m─Б╠Б naс╕е priy─Б╠Бs (TS.TA. priy─Б╠Б m─Б╠Б nas) tanv├▓
rudra r─лriс╣гaс╕е. The comm. has vakс╣гatas in b. тМКPpp. has, for b,
m─Б na kс╣г─лyanta uta m─Б no akс╣гata.тМЛ

Griffith

Harm thou among us neither great nor little, not one who bears us, not our future bearers. Injure no sire among us, harm no mother. Forbear to injure our own bodies, Rudra.

Whitney
Translation
  1. To Rudra’s howl-making, unhymned-swallowing (?), great-mouthed dogs
    I have paid this homage.
Notes

The obscure asaс╣Бs┼лktagil├б (Ppp. -girebhyas) is paraphrased by the
comm. with asam─лc─лnam a┼Ыobhanavacanaс╣Б gс╣Ыс╣Зanti bh─Бс╣гante. How
asaс╣Бs┼лkta should come to mean ‘unmasticated,’ as given in the Pet.
Lexx., does not appear. The translation given conjectures ’not having a
hymn with it.’ The comm. reads elavak- in a.

Griffith

This lowly reverence have I paid to Rudra’s dogs with mighty mouths, Hounds terrible with bark and howl, who gorge unmasticated food.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Homage to thy noisy ones, homage to thy hairy ones, homage to those
    to whom homage is paid, homage to the jointly-enjoyingтАФhomage,
    [namely], O god, to thine armies; welfare [be] to us, and
    fearlessness to us.
Notes

The adjectives are fem., as belonging to sen─Бbhyas. Ppp. disagrees
with our text in the last two thirds of the verse, but is corrupt. The
comm. reads cana at the end. тМКThe vs., as noted above, is quoted in
the first abhaya gaс╣Зa (note to 16. 8).тМЛ

тМКHere ends the first anuv─Бka, with 2 hymns and 68 verses. The quoted
Anukr. says tath─Бi тАЩva r─Бudre тАШpi par─Бs tu viс╣Е┼Ыateс╕е, designating the
hymn as a “Rudra-hymn."тМЛ

Griffith

Homage to thy loud-shouting hosts and thy long-haired followers! Homage to hosts that are adored, homage to armies that enjoy Homage to all thy troops, O God. Security and bliss be ours!
